   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Book_of_the_Description_of_the_Earth&amp;redirect=no" title="Book of the Description of the Earth">
       Book of the Description of the Earth
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       "al-Khwārizmī" redirects here. For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Al-Khw%C4%81rizm%C4%AB_(disambiguation)" title="Al-Khwārizmī (disambiguation)">
        al-Khwārizmī (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <table class="infobox biography vcard" style="width:22em">
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold">
         <span class="fn">
          Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī
         </span>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khwarizmi_Amirkabir_University_of_Technology.png">
          <img alt="Khwarizmi Amirkabir University of Technology.png" data-file-height="553" data-file-width="282" height="441" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Khwarizmi_Amirkabir_University_of_Technology.png/225px-Khwarizmi_Amirkabir_University_of_Technology.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a6/Khwarizmi_Amirkabir_University_of_Technology.png 1.5x" width="225"/>
         </a>
         <div>
          Khwarizmi statute in
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amir_Kabir_University" title="Amir Kabir University">
           Amir Kabir University
          </a>
          , Tehran
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Born
        </th>
        <td>
         c.
         <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
          780
         </span>
         <br/>
         <span class="birthplace">
          <a href="/wiki/Khwarezm" title="Khwarezm">
           Khwarezm
          </a>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-1">
            [1]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Died
        </th>
        <td>
         c.
         <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
          850
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         Academic work
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Era
        </th>
        <td>
         Medieval era (
         <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">
          Islamic Golden Age
         </a>
         )
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Notable ideas
        </th>
        <td>
         Treatises on
         <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">
          algebra
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Indian_numerals" title="Indian numerals">
          Indian numerals
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Influenced
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Kamil" title="Abu Kamil">
          Abu Kamil
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacTutor-AK_2-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-AK-2">
           [2]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="display:none">
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        <i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration" xml:lang="ar-Latn">
         Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī
        </i>
       </b>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">
        Persian
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="fa" xml:lang="fa">
        محمد بن موسى خوارزمی
       </span>
       ‎; c.
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        780
       </span>
       – c.
       <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
        850
       </span>
       ), formerly
       <a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">
        Latinized
       </a>
       as
       <i>
        Algoritmi
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [note 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       was a
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
        Persian
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persian_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Persian-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
        Muslim
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       scholar in the
       <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">
        House of Wisdom
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">
        Baghdad
       </a>
       who produced works in
       <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">
        mathematics
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world">
        astronomy
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_and_cartography_in_medieval_Islam" title="Geography and cartography in medieval Islam">
        geography
       </a>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">
        Abbasid Caliphate
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 12th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       translations of
       <a href="#Arithmetic">
        his work
       </a>
       on the
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_numerals" title="Indian numerals">
        Indian numerals
       </a>
       introduced the
       <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">
        decimal
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Positional_notation" title="Positional notation">
        positional number system
       </a>
       to the Western world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struik_93_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Struik_93-9">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Al-Khwārizmī's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Compendious_Book_on_Calculation_by_Completion_and_Balancing" title="The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing">
         The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing
        </a>
       </i>
       presented the first systematic solution of
       <a href="/wiki/Linear_equation" title="Linear equation">
        linear
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_equation" title="Quadratic equation">
        quadratic equations
       </a>
       in Arabic.Because he is the first to teach algebra as an independent discipline and introduced the methods of "reduction" and "balancing" (the transposition of subtracted terms to the other side of an equation, that is, the cancellation of like terms on opposite sides of the equation), he has been described as the father
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or founder
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">
        algebra
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing
       </i>
       ,translated into Latin by
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_of_Chester" title="Robert of Chester">
        Robert of Chester
       </a>
       in 1145, was used until the sixteenth century as the principal mathematical text-book of
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_medieval_universities" title="List of medieval universities">
        European universities
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       He revised
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">
         Geography
        </a>
       </i>
       and wrote on astronomy and astrology.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some words reflect the importance of al-Khwārizmī's contributions to mathematics. "Algebra" is derived from
       <i>
        al-jabr
       </i>
       , one of the two operations he used to solve quadratic equations.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Algorism" title="Algorism">
         Algorism
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">
         algorithm
        </a>
       </i>
       stem from
       <i>
        Algoritmi
       </i>
       , the Latin form of his name.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His name is also the origin of (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       )
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/guarismo" title="wikt:guarismo">
         guarismo
        </a>
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and of (
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       )
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/algarismo" title="wikt:algarismo">
         algarismo
        </a>
       </i>
       , both meaning
       <a href="/wiki/Numerical_digit" title="Numerical digit">
        digit
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11">

          <span class="tocnumber">
           4
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Notes
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12">
         <a href="#References">
          <span class="tocnumber">

          </span>

          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13">
         <a href="#Further_reading">

          </span>

          </span>
         </a>
         <ul>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14">
           <a href="#Specific_references">

            </span>

            </span>

          </li>

      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Life">
        Life
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Few details of al-Khwārizmī's life are known with certainty. He was born in a
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
        Persian
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Persian_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Persian-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       family and
       <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nadim" title="Ibn al-Nadim">
        Ibn al-Nadim
       </a>
       gives his birthplace as
       <a href="/wiki/Khwarezm" title="Khwarezm">
        Khwarezm
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">
        Greater Khorasan
       </a>
       (modern
       <a href="/wiki/Khiva" title="Khiva">
        Khiva
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Xorazm_Region" title="Xorazm Region">
        Xorazm Region
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">
        Uzbekistan
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Jarir_al-Tabari" title="Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari">
        Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari
       </a>
       gives his name as Muḥammad ibn Musá al-Khwārizmiyy al-
       <a href="/wiki/Majus" title="Majus">
        Majūsiyy
       </a>
       al-Quṭrubbaliyy (
       <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        محمد بن موسى الخوارزميّ المجوسـيّ القطربّـليّ
       </span>
       ). The
       <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">
        epithet
       </a>
       <i>
        al-Qutrubbulli
       </i>
       could indicate he might instead have come from Qutrubbul (Qatrabbul),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a
       <a href="/wiki/Viticulture" title="Viticulture">
        viticulture
       </a>
       district near Baghdad. However, Rashed
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       suggests:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        There is no need to be an expert on the period or a philologist to see that al-Tabari's second citation should read "Muhammad ibn Mūsa al-Khwārizmī
        <i>
         and
        </i>
        al-Majūsi al-Qutrubbulli," and that there are two people (al-Khwārizmī and al-Majūsi al-Qutrubbulli) between whom the letter
        <i>
         wa
        </i>
        [Arabic '
        <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
         و
        </span>
        ' for the conjunction '
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D9%88#Etymology_2" title="wiktionary:و">
         and
        </a>
        '] has been omitted in an early copy. This would not be worth mentioning if a series of errors concerning the personality of al-Khwārizmī, occasionally even the origins of his knowledge, had not been made. Recently,
        <a href="/wiki/Gerald_J._Toomer" title="Gerald J. Toomer">
         G. J. Toomer
        </a>
        ... with naive confidence constructed an entire fantasy on the error which cannot be denied the merit of amusing the reader.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Regarding al-Khwārizmī's religion, Toomer writes:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Another epithet given to him by al-Ṭabarī, "al-Majūsī," would seem to indicate that he was an adherent of the old
        <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">
         Zoroastrian religion
        </a>
        . This would still have been possible at that time for a man of Iranian origin, but the pious preface to al-Khwārizmī's
        <i>
         Algebra
        </i>
        shows that he was an orthodox
        <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
         Muslim
        </a>
        , so al-Ṭabarī's epithet could mean no more than that his forebears, and perhaps he in his youth, had been Zoroastrians.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toomer_24-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-toomer-24">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       However, Rashed put a rather different interpretation on the same words by Al-Tabari:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        ... Al-Tabari's words should read: "Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi and al-Majusi al-Qutrubbulli ...", (and that there are two people al-Khwarizmi and al-Majusi al-Qutrubbulli): the letter "wa" was omitted in the early copy. This would not be worth mentioning if a series of conclusions about al-Khwarizmi's personality, occasionally even the origins of his knowledge, had not been drawn. In his article ([1]) G J Toomer, with naive confidence, constructed an entire fantasy on the error which cannot be denied the merit of making amusing reading.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nadim" title="Ibn al-Nadim">
        Ibn al-Nadīm
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Kitāb al-Fihrist
       </i>
       includes a short biography on al-Khwārizmī together with a list of the books he wrote. Al-Khwārizmī accomplished most of his work in the period between 813 and 833. After the
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">
        Muslim conquest of Persia
       </a>
       , Baghdad became the centre of scientific studies and trade, and many merchants and scientists from as far as
       <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
        China
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
        India
       </a>
       traveled to this city, as did al-Khwārizmī
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       . He worked in Baghdad as a scholar at the House of Wisdom established by
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">
        Caliph al-Ma’mūn
       </a>
       , where he studied the sciences and mathematics, which included the translation of
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">
        Sanskrit
       </a>
       scientific manuscripts.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Morton_Dunlop" title="Douglas Morton Dunlop">
        Douglas Morton Dunlop
       </a>
       suggests that it may have been possible that Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī was in fact the same person as Muḥammad ibn Mūsā ibn Shākir, the eldest of the three
       <a href="/wiki/Ban%C5%AB_M%C5%ABs%C4%81" title="Banū Mūsā">
        Banū Mūsā
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions">
        Contributions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Contributions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="240" height="348" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg/220px-Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A page from al-Khwārizmī's
         <i>
          Algebra
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī's contributions to mathematics, geography, astronomy, and
       <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">
        cartography
       </a>
       established the basis for innovation in algebra and
       <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">
        trigonometry
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       . His systematic approach to solving linear and quadratic equations led to
       <i>
        algebra
       </i>
       , a word derived from the title of his book on the subject, "The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing".
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        On the Calculation with Hindu Numerals
       </i>
       written about 820, was principally responsible for spreading the
       <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">
        Hindu–Arabic numeral system
       </a>
       throughout the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
        Middle East
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       . It was translated into Latin as
       <i>
        Algoritmi de numero Indorum
       </i>
       . Al-Khwārizmī, rendered as (Latin)
       <i>
        Algoritmi
       </i>
       , led to the term "algorithm".
      </p>
      <p>
       Some of his work was based on Persian and
       <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
        Babylonian
       </a>
       astronomy, Indian numbers, and
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">
        Greek mathematics
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī systematized and corrected
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       's data for Africa and the Middle East. Another major book was
       <i>
        Kitab surat al-ard
       </i>
       ("The Image of the Earth"; translated as Geography), presenting the coordinates of places based on those in the
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">
        <i>
         Geography
        </i>
        of Ptolemy
       </a>
       but with improved values for the
       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">
        Mediterranean Sea
       </a>
       , Asia, and Africa.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       He also wrote on mechanical devices like the
       <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">
        astrolabe
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sundial" title="Sundial">
        sundial
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       He assisted a project to determine the circumference of the Earth and in making a world map for
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">
        al-Ma'mun
       </a>
       , the caliph, overseeing 70 geographers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When, in the 12th century, his works spread to Europe through Latin translations, it had a profound impact on the advance of mathematics in Europe.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra">
        Algebra
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Algebra">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/The_Compendious_Book_on_Calculation_by_Completion_and_Balancing" title="The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing">
        The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_the_12th_century" title="Latin translations of the 12th century">
        Latin translations of the 12th century
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">
        Science in the medieval Islamic world
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:258px;max-width:258px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_(Arabic).png">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="369" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png/130px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png/195px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png/260px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png 2x" width="130"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:122px;max-width:122px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_(English).png">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="390" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png/120px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png/180px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png/240px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png 2x" width="120"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
         Left: The original Arabic print manuscript of the
         <i>
          Book of Algebra
         </i>
         by Al-Khwārizmī. Right: A page from
         <i>
          The Algebra of Al-Khwarizmi
         </i>
         by Fredrick Rosen, in
         <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
          English
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        الكتاب المختصر في حساب الجبر والمقابلة
       </span>
       ‎
       <i>
        al-Kitāb al-mukhtaṣar fī ḥisāb al-jabr wal-muqābala
       </i>
       ) is a mathematical book written approximately 820 CE. The book was written with the encouragement of
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">
        Caliph al-Ma'mun
       </a>
       as a popular work on calculation and is replete with examples and applications to a wide range of problems in trade, surveying and legal inheritance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algebra_1831_translation_rosen_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Algebra_1831_translation_rosen-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The term "algebra" is derived from the name of one of the basic operations with equations (
       <i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration" xml:lang="ar-Latn">
        al-jabr
       </i>
       , meaning "restoration", referring to adding a number to both sides of the equation to consolidate or cancel terms) described in this book. The book was translated in Latin as
       <i>
        Liber algebrae et almucabala
       </i>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_of_Chester" title="Robert of Chester">
        Robert of Chester
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Segovia" title="Segovia">
        Segovia
       </a>
       , 1145) hence "algebra", and also by
       <a href="/wiki/Gerard_of_Cremona" title="Gerard of Cremona">
        Gerard of Cremona
       </a>
       . A unique Arabic copy is kept at Oxford and was translated in 1831 by F. Rosen. A Latin translation is kept in Cambridge.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It provided an exhaustive account of solving polynomial equations up to the second degree,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and discussed the fundamental methods of "reduction" and "balancing", referring to the transposition of terms to the other side of an equation, that is, the cancellation of like terms on opposite sides of the equation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyer-229_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Boyer-229-31">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī's method of solving linear and quadratic equations worked by first reducing the equation to one of six standard forms (where
       <i>
        b
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       are positive integers)
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        squares equal roots (
        <i>
         ax
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        =
        <i>
         bx
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        squares equal number (
        <i>
         ax
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        =
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        roots equal number (
        <i>
         bx
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        squares and roots equal number (
        <i>
         ax
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        +
        <i>
         bx
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        squares and number equal roots (
        <i>
         ax
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        +
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         bx
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        roots and number equal squares (
        <i>
         bx
        </i>
        +
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         ax
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       by dividing out the coefficient of the square and using the two operations
       <i>
        al-jabr
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        الجبر
       </span>
       ‎ "restoring" or "completion") and
       <i>
        al-muqābala
       </i>
       ("balancing").
       <i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration" xml:lang="ar-Latn">
        Al-jabr
       </i>
       is the process of removing negative units, roots and squares from the equation by adding the same quantity to each side. For example,
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       = 40
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       − 4
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       is reduced to 5
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       = 40
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       .
       <i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration" xml:lang="ar-Latn">
        Al-muqābala
       </i>
       is the process of bringing quantities of the same type to the same side of the equation. For example,
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       + 14 =
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       + 5 is reduced to
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       + 9 =
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The above discussion uses modern mathematical notation for the types of problems which the book discusses. However, in al-Khwārizmī's day, most of this notation
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_mathematical_notation" title="History of mathematical notation">
        had not yet been invented
       </a>
       , so he had to use ordinary text to present problems and their solutions. For example, for one problem he writes, (from an 1831 translation)
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        If some one say: "You divide ten into two parts: multiply the one by itself; it will be equal to the other taken eighty-one times." Computation: You say, ten less thing, multiplied by itself, is a hundred plus a square less twenty things, and this is equal to eighty-one things. Separate the twenty things from a hundred and a square, and add them to eighty-one. It will then be a hundred plus a square, which is equal to a hundred and one roots. Halve the roots; the moiety is fifty and a half. Multiply this by itself, it is two thousand five hundred and fifty and a quarter. Subtract from this one hundred; the remainder is two thousand four hundred and fifty and a quarter. Extract the root from this; it is forty-nine and a half. Subtract this from the moiety of the roots, which is fifty and a half. There remains one, and this is one of the two parts.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algebra_1831_translation_rosen_28-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Algebra_1831_translation_rosen-28">
          [26]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       In modern notation this process, with 'x' the "thing" (
       <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        شيء
       </span>
       <i>
        shayʾ
       </i>
       ) or "root", is given by the steps,
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle (10-x)^{2}=81x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mn>
               10
              </mn>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <msup>
               <mo stretchy="false">
                )
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mn>
               81
              </mn>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle (10-x)^{2}=81x}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>

        </span>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}-20x+100=81x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>

            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>

        </span>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle x^{2}+100=101x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msup>
               <mi>
                x
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>

              </mo>
              <mn>
               100

              <mo>

              </mo>

              </mn>

              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle x^{2}+100=101x}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="x^{2}+100=101x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ec368c0c20a6fb1ea128e5e0050b59853117c35f" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:16.627ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Let the roots of the equation be 'p' and 'q'. Then
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {p+q}{2}}=50{\tfrac {1}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  p
                 </mi>
                 <mo>
                  +
                 </mo>
                 <mi>

                 </mi>

                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mstyle>
             </mrow>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              50
             </mn>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mstyle>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {\tfrac {p+q}{2}}=50{\tfrac {1}{2}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\tfrac {p+q}{2}}=50{\tfrac {1}{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6f79437cfbda91b263c392f4ef06df7943b15d55" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:10.779ex; height:3.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle pq=100}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>

             </mo>

              100
             </mn>

           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="pq=100" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e932f87bac94a5559705ffdf2412962f95a1a1ce" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:8.914ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       and
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {p-q}{2}}={\sqrt {\left({\frac {p+q}{2}}\right)^{2}-pq}}={\sqrt {2550{\tfrac {1}{4}}-100}}=49{\tfrac {1}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                 </mi>

                 </mo>

                 2

              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <msqrt>
                <msup>
                 <mrow>
                  <mo>
                   (
                  </mo>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mfrac>
                    <mrow>

                     </mi>

                     </mo>

                  <mn>

                </mo>

              <mo>

              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                 2550
                </mn>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                  <mfrac>

                   </mn>

              <mo>

              </mo>

              </mn>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
