                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\frac {p-q}{2}}={\sqrt {\left({\frac {p+q}{2}}\right)^{2}-pq}}={\sqrt {2550{\tfrac {1}{4}}-100}}=49{\tfrac {1}{2}}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\frac {p-q}{2}}={\sqrt {\left({\frac {p+q}{2}}\right)^{2}-pq}}={\sqrt {2550{\tfrac {1}{4}}-100}}=49{\tfrac {1}{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/702c7bb4fca50b5039ad0e1e00050f698c41484b" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:51.947ex; height:7.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       So a root is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle x=50{\tfrac {1}{2}}-49{\tfrac {1}{2}}=1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mn>
               50
              </mn>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                <mfrac>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mn>
               49
              </mn>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                <mfrac>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mn>
               1
              </mn>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle x=50{\tfrac {1}{2}}-49{\tfrac {1}{2}}=1}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="x=50{\tfrac {1}{2}}-49{\tfrac {1}{2}}=1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ae5d6620c87492ec32acd03ec332916e37adfae" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:19.496ex; height:3.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Several authors have also published texts under the name of
       <i>
        Kitāb al-jabr wal-muqābala
       </i>
       , including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_%E1%B8%A4an%C4%ABfa_D%C4%ABnawar%C4%AB" title="Abū Ḥanīfa Dīnawarī">
        Abū Ḥanīfa Dīnawarī
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_K%C4%81mil_Shuj%C4%81%CA%BF_ibn_Aslam" title="Abū Kāmil Shujāʿ ibn Aslam">
        Abū Kāmil Shujāʿ ibn Aslam
       </a>
       , Abū Muḥammad al-‘Adlī, Abū Yūsuf al-Miṣṣīṣī,
       <a href="/wiki/%27Abd_al-Ham%C4%ABd_ibn_Turk" title="'Abd al-Hamīd ibn Turk">
        'Abd al-Hamīd ibn Turk
       </a>
       , Sind ibn ‘Alī, Sahl ibn Bišr, and
       <a href="/wiki/Sharaf_al-D%C4%ABn_al-%E1%B9%AC%C5%ABs%C4%AB" title="Sharaf al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī">
        Sharaf al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       J. J. O'Conner and E. F. Robertson wrote in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/MacTutor_History_of_Mathematics_archive" title="MacTutor History of Mathematics archive">
         MacTutor History of Mathematics archive
        </a>
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Perhaps one of the most significant advances made by
        <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">
         Arabic mathematics
        </a>
        began at this time with the work of al-Khwarizmi, namely the beginnings of algebra. It is important to understand just how significant this new idea was. It was a revolutionary move away from the Greek concept of mathematics which was essentially geometry. Algebra was a unifying theory which allowed
        <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">
         rational numbers
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Irrational_number" title="Irrational number">
         irrational numbers
        </a>
        , geometrical magnitudes, etc., to all be treated as "algebraic objects". It gave mathematics a whole new development path so much broader in concept to that which had existed before, and provided a vehicle for future development of the subject. Another important aspect of the introduction of algebraic ideas was that it allowed mathematics to be applied to itself in a way which had not happened before.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacTutor_32-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-32">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       R. Rashed and Angela Armstrong write:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Al-Khwarizmi's text can be seen to be distinct not only from the
        <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_mathematics" title="Babylonian mathematics">
         Babylonian tablets
        </a>
        , but also from
        <a href="/wiki/Diophantus" title="Diophantus">
         Diophantus
        </a>
        '
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Arithmetica" title="Arithmetica">
          Arithmetica
         </a>
        </i>
        . It no longer concerns a series of
        <a href="/wiki/Problem_solving" title="Problem solving">
         problems to be resolved
        </a>
        , but an
        <a href="/wiki/Rhetorical_modes#Exposition" title="Rhetorical modes">
         exposition
        </a>
        which starts with primitive terms in which the combinations must give all possible prototypes for equations, which henceforward explicitly constitute the true object of study. On the other hand, the idea of an equation for its own sake appears from the beginning and, one could say, in a generic manner, insofar as it does not simply emerge in the course of solving a problem, but is specifically called on to define an infinite class of problems.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
         <a href="#cite_note-33">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       According to Swiss-American historian of mathematics,
       <a href="/wiki/Florian_Cajori" title="Florian Cajori">
        Florian Cajori
       </a>
       ,Al-Khwarizmi's algebra was different from the work of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_mathematicians" title="Indian mathematicians">
        Indian mathematicians
       </a>
       ,for Indians had no rules like the ''restoration'' and ''reduction''.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Regarding dissimilarity and significance of Al-Khwarizmi's algebraic work from that of Indian Mathematician
       <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">
        Brahmagupta
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benjamin_Boyer" title="Carl Benjamin Boyer">
        Carl Benjamin Boyer
       </a>
       write:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        It is quite unlikely that al-Khwarizmi knew of the work of Diophantus, but he must have been familiar with at least the astronomical and computational portions of Brahmagupta; yet neither al-Khwarizmi nor other Arabic scholars made use of syncopation or of negative numbers.Nevertheless,the
        <i>
         Al-jabr
        </i>
        comes closer to elementary algebra of today than the works of either Diophantus or Brahmagupta, because the book is not concerned with difficult problems in indeterminant analysis but with a straight forward and elementary exposition of the solution of equations, especially that of second degree.The Arabs in general loved a good clear argument from premise to conclusion,as well as systematic organization – respects in which neither Diophantus nor the Hindus excelled.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
         <a href="#cite_note-35">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dixit_algorizmi.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="1001" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dixit_algorizmi.png/220px-Dixit_algorizmi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dixit_algorizmi.png/330px-Dixit_algorizmi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dixit_algorizmi.png/440px-Dixit_algorizmi.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dixit_algorizmi.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Page from a Latin translation, beginning with "Dixit algorizmi"
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic">
        Arithmetic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Arithmetic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī's second major work was on the subject of arithmetic, which survived in a Latin translation but was lost in the original Arabic. The translation was most likely done in the 12th century by
       <a href="/wiki/Adelard_of_Bath" title="Adelard of Bath">
        Adelard of Bath
       </a>
       , who had also translated the astronomical tables in 1126.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Latin manuscripts are untitled, but are commonly referred to by the first two words with which they start:
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         <i>
          Dixit algorizmi
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ("So said"), or
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         <i>
          Algoritmi de numero Indorum
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ("al-Khwārizmī on the Hindu Art of Reckoning"), a name given to the work by
       <a href="/wiki/Baldassarre_Boncompagni" title="Baldassarre Boncompagni">
        Baldassarre Boncompagni
       </a>
       in 1857. The original Arabic title was possibly
       <i>
        Kitāb al-Jam‘ wat-Tafrīq bi-Ḥisāb al-Hind
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ("The Book of Addition and Subtraction According to the Hindu Calculation").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī's work on arithmetic was responsible for introducing the
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">
        Arabic numerals
       </a>
       , based on the
       <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">
        Hindu–Arabic numeral system
       </a>
       developed in
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_mathematics" title="Indian mathematics">
        Indian mathematics
       </a>
       , to the Western world. The term "algorithm" is derived from the
       <a href="/wiki/Algorism" title="Algorism">
        algorism
       </a>
       , the technique of performing arithmetic with Hindu-Arabic numerals developed by al-Khwārizmī. Both "algorithm" and "algorism" are derived from the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latinised_names" title="List of Latinised names">
        Latinized forms
       </a>
       of al-Khwārizmī's name,
       <i>
        Algoritmi
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Algorismi
       </i>
       , respectively.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">
        Astronomy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Astronomy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_(1).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1079" data-file-width="751" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png/220px-Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png/330px-Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png/440px-Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_(1).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Page from
         <i>
          Corpus Christi College MS 283
         </i>
         . A Latin translation of al-Khwārizmī's
         <i>
          Zīj
         </i>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī's
       <i>
        Zīj al-Sindhind
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toomer_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-toomer-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        زيج السند هند
       </span>
       ‎, "
       <a href="/wiki/Zij" title="Zij">
        astronomical tables
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Siddhanta#Astronomy" title="Siddhanta">
         Siddhanta
        </a>
       </i>
       "
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thurston1996_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thurston1996-38">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) is a work consisting of approximately 37 chapters on calendrical and astronomical calculations and 116 tables with calendrical, astronomical and astrological data, as well as a table of
       <a href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">
        sine
       </a>
       values. This is the first of many Arabic
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Zij" title="Zij">
         Zijes
        </a>
       </i>
       based on the
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_astronomy" title="Indian astronomy">
        Indian astronomical
       </a>
       methods known as the
       <i>
        sindhind
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy-1956_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-1956-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The work contains tables for the movements of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        sun
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        moon
       </a>
       and the five
       <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">
        planets
       </a>
       known at the time. This work marked the turning point in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_astronomy" title="Islamic astronomy">
        Islamic astronomy
       </a>
       . Hitherto, Muslim astronomers had adopted a primarily research approach to the field, translating works of others and learning already discovered knowledge.
      </p>
      <p>
       The original Arabic version (written c. 820) is lost, but a version by the Spanish astronomer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maslamah_Ibn_Ahmad_al-Majriti" title="Maslamah Ibn Ahmad al-Majriti">
        Maslamah Ibn Ahmad al-Majriti
       </a>
       (c. 1000) has survived in a Latin translation, presumably by
       <a href="/wiki/Adelard_of_Bath" title="Adelard of Bath">
        Adelard of Bath
       </a>
       (January 26, 1126).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The four surviving manuscripts of the Latin translation are kept at the Bibliothèque publique (Chartres), the Bibliothèque Mazarine (Paris), the Biblioteca Nacional (Madrid) and the Bodleian Library (Oxford).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Trigonometry">
        Trigonometry
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Trigonometry">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī's
       <i>
        Zīj al-Sindhind
       </i>
       also contained tables for the
       <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_functions" title="Trigonometric functions">
        trigonometric functions
       </a>
       of sines and cosine.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy-1956_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-1956-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A related treatise on
       <a href="/wiki/Spherical_trigonometry" title="Spherical trigonometry">
        spherical trigonometry
       </a>
       is also attributed to him.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacTutor_32-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-32">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="371" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png/220px-Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png/330px-Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/08/Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hubert_Daunicht&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hubert Daunicht (page does not exist)">
          Daunicht
         </a>
         's reconstruction of the section of al-Khwārizmī's world map concerning the
         <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
          Indian Ocean
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PtolemyWorldMap.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1026" data-file-width="1500" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/220px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/330px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/440px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PtolemyWorldMap.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A
         <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_world_map" title="Ptolemy's world map">
          15th-century version
         </a>
         of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" title="Claudius Ptolemy">
          Ptolemy
         </a>
         's
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_Geography" title="Ptolemy's Geography">
          <i>
           Geography
          </i>
         </a>
         for comparison.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1983_CPA_5426_(1).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="985" data-file-width="735" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png/220px-1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png/330px-1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png/440px-1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1983_CPA_5426_(1).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A
         <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_stamp" title="Commemorative stamp">
          stamp
         </a>
         issued September 6, 1983 in the
         <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
          Soviet Union
         </a>
         , commemorating al-Khwārizmī's (approximate) 1200th birthday.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khiva.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="960" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Khiva.jpg/220px-Khiva.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Khiva.jpg/330px-Khiva.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Khiva.jpg/440px-Khiva.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khiva.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Statue of Al-Khwārizmī in his birth town
         <a href="/wiki/Khiva" title="Khiva">
          Khiva
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">
          Uzbekistan
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī's third major work is his
       <i>
        Kitāb Ṣūrat al-Arḍ
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        كتاب صورة الأرض
       </span>
       ‎, "Book of the Description of the Earth"),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       also known as his
       <i>
        Geography
       </i>
       , which was finished in 833. It is a major reworking of
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       's 2nd-century
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographia_(Ptolemy)" title="Geographia (Ptolemy)">
         Geography
        </a>
       </i>
       , consisting of a list of 2402 coordinates of cities and other geographical features following a general introduction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is only one surviving copy of
       <i>
        Kitāb Ṣūrat al-Arḍ
       </i>
       , which is kept at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strasbourg_University_Library" title="Strasbourg University Library">
        Strasbourg University Library
       </a>
       . A Latin translation is kept at the
       <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Nacional_de_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Biblioteca Nacional de España">
        Biblioteca Nacional de España
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">
        Madrid
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The book opens with the list of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latitudes" title="Latitudes">
        latitudes
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longitudes" title="Longitudes">
        longitudes
       </a>
       , in order of "weather zones", that is to say in blocks of latitudes and, in each
       <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">
        weather
       </a>
       zone, by order of longitude. As
       <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gallez" title="Paul Gallez">
        Paul Gallez
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">
          <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2011)">
           dubious
          </span>
         </a>
         <span class="metadata">
          –
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi#Dubious" title="Talk:Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi">
           discuss
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       points out, this excellent system allows the deduction of many latitudes and longitudes where the only extant document is in such a bad condition as to make it practically illegible. Neither the Arabic copy nor the Latin translation include the map of the world itself; however, Hubert Daunicht was able to reconstruct the missing map from the list of coordinates. Daunicht read the latitudes and longitudes of the coastal points in the manuscript, or deduces them from the context where they were not legible. He transferred the points onto
       <a href="/wiki/Graph_paper" title="Graph paper">
        graph paper
       </a>
       and connected them with straight lines, obtaining an approximation of the coastline as it was on the original map. He then does the same for the rivers and towns.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī corrected Ptolemy's gross overestimate for the length of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">
        Mediterranean Sea
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy-188_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-188-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">
        Canary Islands
       </a>
       to the eastern shores of the Mediterranean; Ptolemy overestimated it at 63 degrees of
       <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">
        longitude
       </a>
       , while al-Khwārizmī almost correctly estimated it at nearly 50 degrees of longitude. He "also depicted the
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">
        Atlantic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
        Indian Oceans
       </a>
       as
       <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">
        open bodies of water
       </a>
       , not land-locked
       <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">
        seas
       </a>
       as Ptolemy had done."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Covington_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Covington-45">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Al-Khwārizmī's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" title="Prime Meridian">
        Prime Meridian
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/Fortunate_Isles" title="Fortunate Isles">
        Fortunate Isles
       </a>
       was thus around 10° east of the line used by Marinus and Ptolemy. Most medieval Muslim gazetteers continued to use al-Khwārizmī's prime meridian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy-188_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-188-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_calendar">
        Jewish calendar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Jewish calendar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Al-Khwārizmī wrote several other works including a treatise on the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_calendar" title="Hebrew calendar">
        Hebrew calendar
       </a>
       , titled
       <i>
        Risāla fi istikhrāj ta’rīkh al-yahūd
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        رسالة في إستخراج تأريخ اليهود
       </span>
       ‎, "Extraction of the Jewish Era"). It describes the
       <a href="/wiki/Metonic_cycle" title="Metonic cycle">
        Metonic cycle
       </a>
       , a 19-year intercalation cycle; the rules for determining on what day of the week the first day of the month
       <a href="/wiki/Tishrei" title="Tishrei">
        Tishrei
       </a>
       shall fall; calculates the interval between the
       <a href="/wiki/Anno_Mundi" title="Anno Mundi">
        Anno Mundi
       </a>
       or Jewish year and the
       <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_era" title="Seleucid era">
        Seleucid era
       </a>
       ; and gives rules for determining the mean longitude of the sun and the moon using the Hebrew calendar. Similar material is found in the works of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Ray%E1%B8%A5%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" title="Abū Rayḥān al-Bīrūnī">
        Abū Rayḥān al-Bīrūnī
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">
        Maimonides
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toomer_24-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-toomer-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_works">
        Other works
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other works">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nadim" title="Ibn al-Nadim">
        Ibn al-Nadim
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Kitāb al-Fihrist
       </i>
       , an index of Arabic books, mentions al-Khwārizmī's
       <i>
        Kitāb al-Taʾrīkh
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        كتاب التأريخ
       </span>
       ‎), a book of annals. No direct manuscript survives; however, a copy had reached
       <a href="/wiki/Nusaybin" title="Nusaybin">
        Nusaybin
       </a>
       by the 11th century, where its
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">
        metropolitan bishop
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mar" title="Mar">
        Mar
       </a>
       Elyas bar Shinaya, found it. Elias's chronicle quotes it from "the death of the Prophet" through to 169 AH, at which point Elias's text itself hits a lacuna.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Several Arabic manuscripts in Berlin, Istanbul, Tashkent, Cairo and Paris contain further material that surely or with some probability comes from al-Khwārizmī. The Istanbul manuscript contains a paper on sundials; the
       <i>
        Fihrist
       </i>
       credits al-Khwārizmī with
       <i>
        Kitāb ar-Rukhāma(t)
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
        كتاب الرخامة
       </span>
       ‎). Other papers, such as one on the determination of the direction of
       <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">
        Mecca
       </a>
       , are on the
       <a href="/wiki/Spherical_astronomy" title="Spherical astronomy">
        spherical astronomy
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Two texts deserve special interest on the
       <a href="/wiki/Morning_width" title="Morning width">
        morning width
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Ma‘rifat sa‘at al-mashriq fī kull balad
       </i>
       ) and the determination of the
       <a href="/wiki/Azimuth" title="Azimuth">
        azimuth
       </a>
       from a height (
       <i>
        Ma‘rifat al-samt min qibal al-irtifā‘
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       He also wrote two books on using and constructing
       <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">
        astrolabes
       </a>
       .
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-3">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-3">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           There is some confusion in the literature on whether al-Khwārizmī's full name is
           <i>
            Abū ʿAbdallāh Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī
           </i>
           or
           <i>
            Abū Ja‘far Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī
           </i>
           . Ibn Khaldun notes in his encyclopedic work: "The first who wrote upon this branch [algebra] was Abu ‘Abdallah al-Khowarizmi, after whom came Abu Kamil Shoja‘ ibn Aslam." (MacGuckin de Slane). (Rosen 1831, pp. xi–xiii) mentions that "[Abu Abdallah Mohammed ben Musa] lived and wrote under the caliphate of Al Mamun, and must therefore be distinguished from Abu Jafar Mohammed ben Musa, likewise a mathematician and astronomer, who flourished under the Caliph Al Motaded (who reigned A.H. 279–289, A.D. 892–902)." In the introduction to his critical commentary on Robert of Chester's Latin translation of al-Khwārizmī's
           <i>
            Algebra
           </i>
           , L.C. Karpinski notes that Abū Ja‘far Muḥammad ibn Mūsā refers to the eldest of the
           <a href="/wiki/Ban%C5%AB_M%C5%ABs%C4%81" title="Banū Mūsā">
            Banū Mūsā
           </a>
           brothers. Karpinski notes in his review on (Ruska 1917) that in (Ruska 1918): "Ruska here inadvertently speaks of the author as Abū Ga‘far M. b. M., instead of Abū Abdallah M. b. M."
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-4">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-4">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Other Latin transliterations include
           <i>
            Algaurizin
           </i>
           .
           <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
            [
            <i>
             <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
              <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">
               citation needed
              </span>
             </a>
            </i>
            ]
           </sup>
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>

         <span class="reference-text">
          (Boyer 1991, "The Arabic Hegemony" p. 229) "It is not certain just what the terms al-jabr and muqabalah mean, but the usual interpretation is similar to that implied in the translation above. The word al-jabr presumably meant something like "restoration" or "completion" and seems to refer to the transposition of subtracted terms to the other side of an equation; the word muqabalah is said to refer to "reduction" or "balancing" - that is, the cancellation of like terms on opposite sides of the equation."
         </span>

         </span>

In a sense, al-Khwarizmi is more entitled to be called "the father of algebra" than Diophantus because al-Khwarizmi is the first to teach algebra in an elementary form and for its own sake, Diophantus is primarily concerned with the theory of numbers."
         </span>

        <li id="cite_note-15">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-15">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           <a href="/wiki/Philip_Khuri_Hitti" title="Philip Khuri Hitti">
            Philip Khuri Hitti
           </a>
           (2002).
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.co.in/books?id=lQbcCwAAQBAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;source=gbs_ge_summary_r&amp;cad=0#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             History of the Arabs
            </i>
           </a>
           . p. 379.
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=History+of+the+Arabs&amp;rft.pages=379&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.au=Philip+Khuri+Hitti&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.co.in%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DlQbcCwAAQBAJ%26printsec%3Dfrontcover%26source%3Dgbs_ge_summary_r%26cad%3D0%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMuhammad+ibn+Musa+al-Khwarizmi">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-16">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-16">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Fred James Hill, ‎Nicholas Awde (2003).
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.co.in/books?id=9S0XAQAAIAAJ" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             A History of the Islamic World
            </i>
           </a>
           . p. 55.
           <q>
            "The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing" (Hisab al-Jabr wa H-Muqabala) on the development of the subject cannot be underestimated. Translated into Latin during the twelfth century, it remained the principal mathematics textbook in European universities until the sixteenth century
           </q>
          </cite>
          <i>

        <li id="cite_note-30">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-30">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benjamin_Boyer" title="Carl Benjamin Boyer">
            Boyer, Carl B.
           </a>
           (1991). "The Arabic Hegemony".
           <i>
            A History of Mathematics
           </i>
           (Second ed.). John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc. p. 228.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-471-54397-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-471-54397-7">
            0-471-54397-7
           </a>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=The+Arabic+Hegemony&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+Mathematics&amp;rft.pages=228&amp;rft.edition=Second&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons%2C+Inc.&amp;rft.date=1991&amp;rft.isbn=0-471-54397-7&amp;rft.aulast=Boyer&amp;rft.aufirst=Carl+B.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMuhammad+ibn+Musa+al-Khwarizmi">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
         <blockquote class="templatequote">
          <p>
           "The Arabs in general loved a good clear argument from premise to conclusion, as well as systematic organization — respects in which neither Diophantus nor the Hindus excelled."
          </p>
         </blockquote>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-Boyer-229-31">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Boyer-229_31-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          (
          <a href="#CITEREFBoyer1991">
           Boyer 1991
          </a>
          , "The Arabic Hegemony" p. 229) "It is not certain just what the terms
          <i>
           al-jabr
          </i>
          and
          <i>
           muqabalah
          </i>
          mean, but the usual interpretation is similar to that implied in the translation above. The word
          <i>
           al-jabr
          </i>
          presumably meant something like "restoration" or "completion" and seems to refer to the transposition of subtracted terms to the other side of an equation; the word
          <i>
           muqabalah
          </i>
          is said to refer to "reduction" or "balancing" — that is, the cancellation of like terms on opposite sides of the equation."
         </span>
        </li>

           . p. 103.
           <q>
            That it came from Indian source is impossible,for Hindus had no rules like "restoration" and "reduction" .They were never in the habit of making all terms in an equation positive, as is done in the process of "restoration.
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+Mathematics&amp;rft.pages=103&amp;rft.date=1919&amp;rft.au=Florian+Cajori&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fahistorymathema02cajogoog&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMuhammad+ibn+Musa+al-Khwarizmi">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>

         <span class="reference-text">
          The full title is "The Book of the Description of the Earth, with its Cities, Mountains, Seas, All the Islands and the Rivers, written by Abu Ja'far Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwārizmī, according to the Geographical Treatise written by Ptolemy the Claudian", although due to ambiguity in the word
          <i>
           surah
          </i>
          it could also be understood as meaning "The Book of the Image of the Earth" or even "The Book of the Map of the World".
